Player_Black (to Proxy):

This is a sharp tactical position. Our knight on b4 is currently attacked by White's bishop on a3, but we have a powerful intermediate move that completely swings the game in our favor.

By playing the knight to d3, we capture a pawn with check and force White into a terrible situation. Let's analyze the consequences:

1... **Nxd3+** (b4d3)
This forks the King on e1. White is forced to respond to the check. There are no good ways to deal with this:
- If White plays **2. Bxd3** (f1xd3), we simply recapture with our pawn **2... exd3**. We have successfully traded our Knight for a Bishop and a Pawn, putting us ahead in material (+1). Furthermore, our new pawn on d3 is a massive thorn in White's position, heavily restricting their pieces.
- If White decides to recapture with the pawn **2. exd3** (e2xd3), this is even worse! It clears the e2 square, and we follow up with **2... exd3+**. This creates a devastating discovered check from our Rook on e8 against the White King on e1. White would lose substantial material trying to block or escape this attack.
- If White simply moves the King (e.g., **2. Kd1**), we save our knight, keeping the free pawn and leaving White's King permanently displaced and vulnerable.

This sequence removes the defender of the center, gains material, and creates either a strong passed pawn or a crushing attack.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To make the best move, let's analyze the current board state and look for tactical opportunities. 

Right now, as Black, you have a massive material advantage, but there's a brilliantly straightforward tactical sequence to win even more material. 

Notice White's Rook on `c4` and White's King on `f1`. Both of these pieces sit exactly on the `a4-e8` diagonal (specifically the `b5-f1` stretch). 
If we move our light-squared Bishop from `d7` to `b5`, we completely paralyze the White Rook.

Let's look at the consequences of **d7b5**:
1. Your Bishop on `b5` will directly attack the White Rook on `c4`.
2. The Rook on `c4` is absolutely pinned to the White King on `f1`. Since Rooks cannot move diagonally, it has zero legal moves to step out of the pin!
3. White has no pieces that can step in to block the pin on `d3` or `e2`. White's only legal response to save the King from the pin logic is to step the King away (e.g., `Kg2` or `Ke1`), which abandons the paralyzed Rook entirely. 
4. On the following turn, you just take the free Rook with your Bishop (`Bxc4`).

This immediately nets you a clean Rook on top of your already crushing advantage! 

make_move d7b5

- `d4` is covered by your `c5` pawn.

3. **... Qe4#**
The Queen steps forward for the final blow. The White King is entirely boxed in by its own pieces (`d1` Queen, `d2` Pawn, `f1` Bishop) and yours (`b4` Knight covers `d3`, `f5` Knight covers `e3`, and the `g3` Bishop slices across the `f2` and `e1` escape squares). It's a pristine checkmate!

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To find the best move, we need to look for forcing checks that exploit the exposed White King on e2. Let's analyze the checks available to Black:

1. **d5e4+** 
The Queen moves to e4 to deliver check. However, White can block this check by playing **c1e3** (Bishop to e3), which is protected by the d2 pawn. If Black follows up with **f5d4+**, the White Bishop on e3 now controls d4 and can simply capture the knight (**e3xd4**).

2. **f5d4+!** 
This is a devastating Knight check. 
Let's see White's responses to this. The Knight on d4 cannot be captured by any White piece (the d1 Queen is blocked by its own d2 pawn). Since it's a Knight check, it cannot be blocked either. The White King must move.

Let's look at the escape squares for the White King currently on e2:
- **d1**, **d2**, and **f1** are occupied by White's own pieces.
- **e1** and **f2** are controlled by Black's dark-squared Bishop on g3.
- **d3** is controlled by Black's Knight on b4.
- **f3** will be controlled by the newly placed Knight on d4.

White's King is completely boxed in and has only one legal square to step to: **Ke3**.

After White is forced into playing **Ke3**, Black has a brutal follow-up: **b4c2+** (or **d4c2+**). 
This secondary Knight jump delivers another check while simultaneously attacking the White Queen on d1 and the Rook on a1. The c2 square is only guarded by the White Queen, meaning White's best option will be to give up their Queen for the Knight (**d1xc2**). Once the Queen captures, Black's other Knight replaces it (**d4xc2+**), restoring the check and the fork against the King and the Rook. Black wins overwhelming material in this forced sequence. 

I'll initiate this winning combination.

make_move f5d4

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To determine the best move, let's analyze the position and look for forcing tactical opportunities.

The White King is aggressively placed on `e3`, making it highly exposed to checks. We have a powerful cluster of pieces in the center, particularly our Knights on `b4` and `d4`, and our Queen on `d5`. 

Let's evaluate the Knight jumps to `c2`:
Both **1... Nd4xc2+** and **1... Nb4xc2+** are available and create a royal fork. A Knight on `c2` will check the White King on `e3`, simultaneously attack the White Queen on `d1` (if she doesn't move/capture), and threaten the White Rook on `a1`. 

Why **1... Nd4xc2+** is the strongest choice over `Nb4xc2+`:
When the `d4` Knight moves, it completely opens up the d-file for your Queen on `d5`. This functionally takes away the `d3` escape square from the White King because your Queen will now control it.
With `d3` restricted, White is forced into one of two terrible responses to the check:

**Scenario A: White plays 2. Qd1xc2**
If White decides to capture the checking Knight with their Queen, they give up the Queen. You would immediately respond with **2... Nb4xc2+**, capturing the Queen, re-forking the King, and ultimately winning the Rook on `a1` on the following move (after the King steps to `e2`). You trade two Knights for a Queen, a Rook, and a Pawn. An overwhelming material advantage!

**Scenario B: White plays 2. Ke3-e2**
Since `Kd3` is strictly illegal (due to your Queen locking down the file), the King must step back to `e2`. If White chooses not to sacrifice their Queen and instead moves their King, you follow up cleanly with **2... Nc2xa1**, picking up the free Rook and a Pawn for nothing. 

Both lines are completely devastating for White.

Action:
make_move d4c2

Currently, White's King is on a1 and its only potential escape squares are a2 and b1. If we capture the b2 pawn with the Rook, we cut off both of these squares.

**make_move d2b2**

Let's examine the board:
White's Queen is currently sitting on `c3`. Black's dark-squared Bishop is on `c5`. 

If you play your Bishop to `b4` (`c5b4`), it attacks the White Queen. Let's look at White's possible responses to save the Queen:
1. **Qxb4:** If White captures the Bishop, the Queen is drawn to the `b4` square and leaves the defense of the `c2` square. Black can then unleash a lethal Royal Fork with `Nd4-c2+`. The Knight forks the White King on `e1` and the White Queen on `b4`. After the King moves out of check, Black plays `Nxb4`, winning a Queen for a Bishop.
2. **Qa3:** If the Queen tries to sidestep the attack to `a3`, the same `Nd4-c2+` strikes again, this time forking the King on `e1` and the Queen on `a3`!
3. **Qb3:** If the Queen goes to `b3`, it simply walks into the direct line of fire of your Knight on `d4` (`Nd4xb3`). 
4. **Qc4 / Qc5 / Qc6:** Moving up the c-file is just as disastrous. `Qc4` is covered by Black's other Bishop on `b5`. `Qc5` is covered by the `d6` pawn. `Qc6` is covered by the `d4` Knight.
5. **Qe3 / Qf3:** Retreating horizontally is impossible because White's own pawn on `d3` blocks the Queen's path.

White's Queen has absolutely no safe squares to escape to and no way to avoid a massive loss of material!

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent forced mate sequence is available here. Let's break it down!

Currently, your Queen on f3 is eyeing the White King on c2. Moving the Queen to d3 delivers a powerful, protected check that will lead to a swift checkmate. 

1. **f3d3+**
By playing this, your Queen is placed directly adjacent to the White King. Let's see why this is so devastating for White:
- The White Pawn on d2 cannot capture your Queen since pawns only capture diagonally, and it is blocked straight ahead.
- The White King cannot capture the Queen on d3 because it is heavily protected by your Rook on d8 down the open d-file.
- Because the Queen is adjacent to the King, White cannot block the check.
- The King's escape squares are completely smothered. Moving to b1 or sliding to c3 leaves him on squares still controlled by your d3 Queen. Your e2 Pawn covers d1. His own Bishop on b2 and Pawns on b3 and d2 block his other escapes.

White's **only** legal move is to slide down to **Kc1**.

2. **e2e1=Q#** (or e2e1=R#)
After the King retreats to c1, you simply push your e2 pawn forward to e1 and promote it to a Queen. This delivers check along the 1st rank. White's King is trapped on c1, blocked by his own pawn and bishop, and pinned down by your d3 Queen which perfectly covers c2 and b1. White will have no pieces that can capture the new e1 Queen or block the check on d1. It's a beautiful, unpreventable checkmate in 2!

I will proceed with the first move of this combination.

make_move f3d3

An excellent positional and tactical choice here is to strike at White's pawn chain with the queenside pawn break. White's setup is currently very cramped and quite behind in development, with the King still in the center.

By playing **b5-b4**, you immediately challenge the `c3` pawn, which is the crucial defender of White's `d4` pawn.

If White accepts the challenge and plays `cxb4`, you recapture with `Nxb4!`. This places your Knight on a tremendously powerful outpost where it eyes the d3 square (threatening a vicious Nd3+ fork) and applies pressure on White's awkwardly placed Ra2. Furthermore, White cannot easily kick the Knight away, and if they try to trade it with `Bxb4`, your dark-squared Bishop recaptures (`Bxb4+`), delivering a check to the uncastled King and seizing total control of the dark squares.

Currently, Black is completely winning, being up a Queen for a Rook. White's position is incredibly cramped, and their King is stuck on e1, babysitting the d2 Knight, which is currently under pressure by our b2 Rook.

The move **e7g5** (moving the dark-squared Bishop to g5) is an excellent choice. It activates the Bishop, takes control of the c1-h6 diagonal, and applies further indirect pressure. From g5, the Bishop is perfectly safe, and if White decides to play `Nc4` attacking the b2 Rook anyway, we simply have the time to slide the Rook over to safety (e.g., to c2 or b6). 

Let's keep the massive material advantage clean and improve our piece coordination.

White's Knight on d6 is currently their most active and bothersome piece, putting pressure on multiple squares. Fortunately, it is undefended and has limited escape routes.

Let's attack it!

**Action:**
`make_move b6c5`

**Explanation:**
By sliding your Queen diagonally to **c5**, you directly attack the undefended d6 Knight. 
Notice how perfectly placed your Queen and pawns are:
- If the Knight tries to escape to **c4** or **e4**, your **d5** pawn has both squares covered and will simply capture it.
- If the Knight tries to hop to **b5** or **f5**, your Queen now covers the entire 5th rank and will snatch it off the board.
- The backward squares **c8** and **e8** are securely covered by your Rooks.

The Knight is virtually trapped. White's only way to save it is to take the b7 pawn (`Nxb7`), sending their only active piece to the edge of the board where it poses no real threat anymore, solidifying your completely winning position.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current board shows that your Queen on a1 is under direct attack by White's Rook on f1. They share the same first rank with no pieces in between to block the threat, which means your Queen must be moved immediately to avoid being captured. 

Let's evaluate the safest and most advantageous flight squares:
- Moving across the 1st rank (`a1b1`, `a1c1`, `a1d1`, `a1e1`) still leaves the Queen on the same rank as the Rook.
- `a1f1` would sacrifice the Queen for the Rook as White's King at e2 can recapture. 
- `a1c3` loses the Queen to White's dark-squared Bishop on d2. 
- `a1d4` moves straight into the path of White's e3 pawn.
- `a1e5` is a safe, centralized option, but White can kick your Queen away by playing `f4` on their next turn, gaining a tempo.

This leaves **a1b2** as the most active and robust move. Moving to b2 pulls your Queen out of the Rook's line of sight to an unprotected dark square. Additionally, it targets White's hanging pawn on c2, creating an immediate problem for White to solve.

make_move a1b2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To determine the best move, let's evaluate the position and the tactical opportunities available.

Looking closely at the board, we can observe the following:
- You have a pawn on **c3** and your Queen is actively placed on **b2**.
- White has a Bishop on **d2**, a King on **e2**, and a Rook on **d1**.

Your pawn on **c3** is attacking the White Bishop on **d2** (since pawns capture one square diagonally forward, and from Black's perspective, c3 to d2 is a forward diagonal step). 

If you play `c3d2`, your pawn simply captures the Bishop for free! Let's calculate White's possible responses:
1. If White tries to recapture the pawn with the Rook (`d1xd2`), the Rook will step onto the d2 square. Because your Queen is safely positioned on the same rank at b2 with an open line of sight to d2, you can then immediately play `b2xd2+`, winning the Rook as well.
2. If White tries to recapture the pawn with their King (`e2xd2`), it is an illegal move because your Queen on b2 is guarding the d2 square along the 2nd rank. 

Therefore, White has no safe way to recapture the pawn. By playing `c3d2`, you cleanly win a Bishop (3 points of material) and leave yourself with an incredibly dangerous, heavily protected passed pawn right on the doorstep of promotion. 

make_move c3d2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the position, you have a beautiful forced checkmate in 2 moves by sacrificing nothing and creating a mating net around the White King.

The White King on f3 is severely restricted, with escape squares covered by your Knights (f6 covers e4 and g4) and the Black Queen on b2 (covering e2 and f2 from a distance). The only square giving the White King some breathing room is the dark square g3. 

By capturing the pawn on g2 with your Rook, you remove this pawn while also taking control of the entire g-file, cutting off the King's escape to g3. At the same time, clearing the d2 square completely opens the 2nd rank for your Queen.

White will be put into *Zugzwang*. Since White has no other pieces except pawns and a trapped King, White is forced to push a pawn (either `e3e4`, `f4f5`, or `h4h5`). No matter which pawn White pushes, the King has no escape. On the next move, you will deliver a protected checkmate right in the King's face!

Here is the line:
1... Rxg2 (`d2g2`) 
2. e4 (or any other pawn move) Qf2# (`b2f2#`) 

The Queen will be perfectly safe on f2, supported laterally by your Rook on g2, checkmating the White King.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the position, Black has a very powerful and forcing pawn push in the center: **e5-e4**.

Here's a breakdown of why this move is excellent:
1. **The Fork:** Pushing your pawn to `e4` directly forks White's light-squared Bishop on `d3` and the pawn on `f3`. 
2. **Undermining the Defense:** If White captures your pawn with `fxe4`, you simply recapture with your d-pawn (`d5xe4`). This forces White's Bishop to retreat (e.g., to `c4` or `e2`). More importantly, removing White's `f3` pawn leaves their advanced `g4` pawn completely unprotected, creating a massive target for your `e6` Bishop or `f6` Knight. If the `f3` pawn is removed, `Bxg4` directly attacks White's Queen on `e2`. 
3. **If White ignores it:** If White attempts to counter-attack (like pushing `g4-g5` to attack your Knight), you simply capture their Bishop (`exd3`). Your `d3` pawn would then be directly attacking their Queen on `e2`, acting as a crushing intermediate threat. White has no time for a counter-attack and will lose material.

It wins space, dictates the tempo, and creates serious tactical problems for White's Kingside.

make_move e5e4

Player_Black (to Proxy):

You're presented with a highly advantageous tactical opportunity! Let's break down the board to find the best move.

Currently, your pawn on **e4** is diagonally adjacent to White's Bishop on **d3**. By capturing it, you not only win a full minor piece for a pawn (net +2 material advantage) but you'll also be setting up a devastating follow-up. 

When your pawn lands on **d3**, it directly attacks White's Queen on **e2**. This forces White to immediately respond to the threat on their Queen (either by moving the Queen or spending a turn to recapture the pawn with `cxd3` or `Qxd3`). 

Because White is forced to respond to your tempo-gaining pawn, they won't have time to save their Rook on **h2**, which is completely exposed on the long diagonal to your dark-squared Bishop on **d6**. No matter what White plays to save their Queen, your next move will likely be `d6xh2`, allowing you to snatch a clean Rook as well!

Let's grab the free Bishop and fork the Queen.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An analysis of the board and legal moves reveals that White has left both their Knight on d5 and their Rook on h2 completely undefended. 

While taking the Knight with `f6d5` or `e6d5` cleanly wins 3 points, taking the Rook with your light-squared Bishop (`d6h2`) wins 5 points. 

At first glance, it might look like capturing the Rook on h2 removes your Bishop from the defense of the c7 pawn, seemingly allowing a nasty Knight fork with `Nxc7` (attacking your a8 Rook and e6 Bishop). However, this is a tactical illusion! Because the squares h2, g3, f4, e5, d6, and c7 all sit on the exact same diagonal, your Bishop on h2 would maintain a direct line of sight all the way back to c7. If White tries the `Nxc7` fork, you can simply reply with `Bxc7`, winning the Knight and maintaining your massive material advantage. Furthermore, White cannot easily trap your Bishop on h2 without losing even more material.

Since capturing the Rook is the most punishing and materially advantageous move, that is the best continuation.

As Black, your best response is to recapture the pawn on d5. The most principled choice here is to recapture with the e-pawn (**exd5**), which establishes a strong central presence and is characteristic of structures like the Tarrasch Defense. 

Recapturing with the Knight (Nxd5) would relinquish central control and allow White to seize space with an eventual e2-e4. Playing cxd4 instead would leave White with a dangerous central pawn on d5. By playing exd5, you maintain a strong foothold in the center, open up lines for your light-squared bishop, and prepare for natural development (such as ...Bxc5 if White ever plays dxc5).

The Bishop on f5 can drop down to c2 to deliver a check. What makes this move so powerful is that the c2 square is perfectly protected by your d4 Knight, meaning the White King cannot just capture the Bishop. If White plays **Kd1-c1** or **Kd1-d2**, you simply follow up with **Nd4-b3+**, forking the King and the undefended White Queen on a1, winning the Queen on the spot. If White opts for **Kd1-e1** to avoid the fork, their King is stranded in the center, and you can continue your massive attack (for instance, with Bb4+ or Nb3 anyway, leaving White completely paralyzed).

An analysis of the position reveals that White has clamped down on the queenside with the strong c5 pawn, supported by the awkward but restricting placement of the Knight on a4. 

However, you can immediately challenge this setup and seize the initiative with a classic pawn thrust: **b7-b5**. 

Here is why this move is excellent:
1. **Direct Attack**: The pawn on b5 directly attacks the White Knight on a4.
2. **Forcing a Decision**: White is practically forced to respond to the attack. They cannot easily support the Knight without losing it (e.g., if 2. b4? then 2... bxa4 wins the piece).
3. **Breaking the Bind**: If White attempts to resolve the tension by capturing *en passant* (2. cxb6 e.p.), you simply recapture with the a-pawn (2... axb6). This achieves multiple strategic goals at once: it permanently removes the annoying c5 pawn, opens up the a-file for your Rook to apply pressure on the a4 Knight, and solidifies your queenside.
4. **Gaining Space**: If White instead retreats the Knight (e.g., 2. Nb2), your b5 pawn becomes a tremendous asset. It solidifies your hold on the dark squares, firmly protects your strong central d4 pawn (if ...a6 is played next), and leaves White's Knight passively placed.

This temporary pawn sacrifice/challenge resolves all your strategic issues on the queenside while dictating the flow of the game.

Based on the current board state and evaluating the tactical possibilities, the move **`g2h1q`** is by far the strongest choice. 

Here's a breakdown of the evaluation:
1. **The Double Threat**: Your Black Bishop on `g4` is currently attacking the unprotected White Queen on `d1`. Meanwhile, your passed pawn on `g2` is attacking the White Rook on `h1` and the White Bishop on `f1`, threatening to promote.
2. **Taking the Free Material**: If you immediately capture the Queen with `g4d1`, White responds with `Bxg2`, extinguishing your promotion threat. You win the Queen but lose your powerful passed pawn (+8 advantage).
3. **The Devastating Promotion**: If you play `g2h1q`, you capture the `h1` Rook and promote to a brand new Queen. Because the White Bishop on `f1` blocks the White Queen on `d1` from seeing `h1`, your newly minted Queen is completely untouchable!
4. **Forced Response**: White is now forced to save their Queen on `d1` by playing `Qxg4` (taking your `g4` Bishop). This creates an overwhelming material swing where you traded a Bishop and a Pawn for a Rook and a brand new Queen (+10 point net swing for you). 
5. **The Follow Up**: Better yet, your new Queen on `h1` will then be able to freely capture the completely undefended `f1` Bishop with check (`Qxf1+`), winning you even more material on the next turn.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

This is a fantastic position for Black. White has played extremely passively and poorly in the opening, leaving their king stranded in the center, failing to develop their queenside minor pieces, and placing their queen awkwardly on a3. Furthermore, White's kingside structure is weakened and the knight on h4 is misplaced.

To exploit an underdeveloped opponent whose king is stuck in the center, the most principled and effective strategy is to blast the center open! 

By playing **d5-d4**, you directly challenge White's pawn structure and prepare to open lines.

1. **If White plays cxd4**: You recapture with `...exd4`. This brilliantly opens the e-file for your rook (Re8 is coming with tempo), while your new d4-wedge acts as a bone in White's throat, permanently paralyzing their d2 and e2 pawns, which blocks the development of their queenside pieces. 
2. **If White pushes b5**: You can simply slide your knight to safety with `...Na5`. The tension remains, your knight finds an annoying outpost, and the threat on c3 persists, ensuring White's structure will crumble.
3. **If White ignores it**: You can capture `...dxc3` on the next turn, further blowing up the position to your massive advantage.

I need to move the attacked c6 Knight to a safe square. Looking at the options:
- `c6e7` looks like a natural retreat, but White's dark-squared Bishop on a3 is eyeing the a3-f8 diagonal. The e7 square lies perfectly on this unobstructed diagonal, so `c6e7` would blunder the Knight to `Bxe7`.

- Retreating to the back rank with `c6d8` or `c6b8` is safe, but arguably passive. 
- Jumping to the edge with `c6a5` safely dodges the pawn's attack and is a very standard maneuver in such pawn-storm structures (often aiming to later reroute or pressure c4/b3). The a5 square is completely safe from White's pawns and pieces.

Given this analysis, I will move the Knight to safety on a5.

To determine the best move, let's analyze the current board and potential threats.

**White's Threats:**
White's dark-squared Bishop on `a3` has a clear, unobstructed view of your Rook on `f8` along the `a3-f8` diagonal (the empty squares are `b4`, `c5`, `d6`, and `e7`). If ignored, White will simply play `Bxf8` and win a full Rook. 

**Candidate Moves:**
You could move the Rook to safety with a move like `f8e8` or `f8d8`. However, moving the Rook defensively is passive and allows White to maintain their strong, uncontested Bishop on that diagonal.

A far better and more thematic response is to completely blunt the Bishop by blocking the diagonal with a pawn while simultaneously claiming central space:

**The Move: c7c5**
By pushing your `c7` pawn two squares to `c5`, you achieve multiple goals:
1. **Blocks the Threat:** The pawn safely lands exactly on the `a3-f8` diagonal, breaking the attack on your Rook. 
2. **Defended Square:** The `c5` square is heavily reinforced. Both your Queen on `b5` and your Bishop on `b6` protect it. If White tries to force the issue with `Bxc5`, you'll easily recapture (`Qxc5` or `Bxc5`), winning a Bishop for a pawn.
3. **Solidifies the Center:** The `c5` pawn acts as a solid anchor, defending and reinforcing your advanced `d4` pawn, giving you an impenetrable pawn chain in the center. 

Since you are already up a massive amount of material (a Queen and a Knight), making solid, improving moves that shut down your opponent's only active counterplay is the easiest path to victory.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent position for Black. The white king is severely restricted on g2, completely boxed in by your powerful g3 pawn and their own pawns. The critical issue for White right now is that their knight on c3 is attacking your queen on b5. Moving the queen is forced, but we can do it with a devastating tempo.

I'll play **b5d3**.

Here's why this move is crushing:
1. **Safety and Centralization:** The queen slides safely down the light-squared diagonal to an unassailable central outpost. There is no white piece that can attack her here.
2. **A Double Threat:** By placing the queen on d3, you immediately attack the undefended d2-pawn, threatening `... Qxd2+`. 
3. **The Mating Net:** If White ignores the d2 pawn (for example, by playing a knight jump like `Nd5`), you will play `... Qxd2+`. Since your g3 pawn covers h2 and f2, your e6 bishop covers h3, and your d4 knight covers f3, the White king will be forced to the 1st rank (f1 or g1). Then, `... Qf2#` is a forced checkmate.
4. **Tactical Synergy:** Even if White tries to defend the d2 pawn with a move like `Ne4` (which defends d2), it relinquishes the defense of f3. This would allow you to sacrifice/capture on f3 with your centralized knight `... Nxf3+!`. The white king wouldn't be able to capture back since your newly positioned queen on d3 protects the f3 square! 

White's position will completely collapse under this pressure.
